<div id="review_1799843524" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1799843524" />
    <a title="Alex" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3144945-alex"><img alt="Alex" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594856152p2/3144945.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1799843524?book_show_action=true">Nov 10, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alex" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alex" href="/user/show/3144945-alex">Alex</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781590173763" href="/book/show/9267777-dead-souls">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Russians who own gold chains</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=2016">2016</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1799843524" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer241333669283431558">Here's a Russian douchebag.<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1478791050i/21112455.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>This is called <i>poshlust,</i> an untranslatable word referring to a kind of banal tackiness special to Russia. Here's another Russian douchebag:<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1478791050i/21112456._SX540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>The stereotype goes all the way back to 1842 and Gogol's great antihero dandy grifter Chichikov, with his Navarino smoke-and-flame silk frock coat and his violet-scented snuffbox, and according to Nabokov <i>poshlust</i> is the great theme of this book, a definition of an essential theme of Russian character.<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1478791050i/21112457._SY540_.jpg' width='400' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><i>Chichikov</i><br/><br/>That's not what Gogol </span>
  <span id="freeText241333669283431558" style="display:none">Here's a Russian douchebag.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1478791050i/21112455.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>This is called <i>poshlust,</i> an untranslatable word referring to a kind of banal tackiness special to Russia. Here's another Russian douchebag:<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1478791050i/21112456._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The stereotype goes all the way back to 1842 and Gogol's great antihero dandy grifter Chichikov, with his Navarino smoke-and-flame silk frock coat and his violet-scented snuffbox, and according to Nabokov <i>poshlust</i> is the great theme of this book, a definition of an essential theme of Russian character.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1478791050i/21112457._SY540_.jpg" width="400" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><i>Chichikov</i><br><br>That's not what Gogol thought <i>Dead Souls</i> was about. He thought he was recreating the Divine Comedy; a morality tale, with three books corresponding to Inferno, Purgatory and Paradise. He only finished the first one: in one of the great tantrums of literature, he burned most of his draft for the rest and then starved himself to death. Lucky for us, Inferno is always the good part.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1478791050i/21112458._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><i>Gogol with his emo face on</i><br><br>The fragments that survive of the rest of <i>Dead Souls,</i> like the ending of <i>Crime &amp; Punishment,</i> get a lot less fun in a hurry. This is the thing about tales of redemption: the redemption is definitely not the fun part. But it's the first great Russian novel, and you can see prototypes here for <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/969751617" title="My review of Crime and Punishment. Bonus: Jack Chick!" rel="nofollow noopener">Raskolnikov</a> and Tolstoy's great conflicted landowner <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/94650129" title="My review of Anna Karenina" rel="nofollow noopener">Levin.</a><br><br>Book One of <i>Dead Souls,</i> which is about two thirds of what we have, is <i>awesome.</i> Vivid, surreal, funny, almost silly, as Gogol is. He's dead serious under that, of course, as they always are. Here's close enough to a mission statement:<blockquote>Some wondrous power has doomed me for a long time to walk hand in hand with my strange heroes, to survey in its entirety life that rushes along so massively, to survey it through laughter that is visible to the world and through tears which the world cannot see and does not know.</blockquote>Unfinished books are <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1469344209" title="My review of Kafka" rel="nofollow noopener">always frustrating,</a> and I didn't enjoy the fragments after Book One. But that first bit is one of my favorite reading experiences this year. This is the great epic of Russian douchebaggery. Unbutton the top four buttons of your silk shirt and get psyched.</span>
  <a data-text-id="241333669283431558" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2618475937" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2618475937" />
    <a title="Antonomasia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6249204-antonomasia"><img alt="Antonomasia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605185148p2/6249204.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2618475937?book_show_action=true">Nov 28, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Antonomasia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Antonomasia" href="/user/show/6249204-antonomasia">Antonomasia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780300060997" href="/book/show/119075.Dead_Souls">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=19th-century">19th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=guardian-1000">guardian-1000</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=russia">russia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=2019">2019</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=1001-books">1001-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=divine-comedy-booklovers">divine-comedy-booklovers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=ukraine">ukraine</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2618475937" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1472999534273424293"><b>5 stars for the B.G. Guerney translation (revised version), Yale UP.<br/>I also read Part II in Donald Rayfield's translation (NYRB Classics / Alma Classics)</b>.<br/><br/>'Exuberant', 'panoramic' and 'state of the nation' are attributes that come up time and again among my favourite novels, so it's hardly surprising I liked this. <i>Dead Souls</i>, in the Guerney translation, is also one of the funniest 19th century novels I've read. <i>Diary of a Nobody</i> was the funniest; I wasn't as keen on <i>Three Men in a Boat</i> as some peo</span>
  <span id="freeText1472999534273424293" style="display:none"><b>5 stars for the B.G. Guerney translation (revised version), Yale UP.<br />I also read Part II in Donald Rayfield's translation (NYRB Classics / Alma Classics)</b>.<br /><br />'Exuberant', 'panoramic' and 'state of the nation' are attributes that come up time and again among my favourite novels, so it's hardly surprising I liked this. <i>Dead Souls</i>, in the Guerney translation, is also one of the funniest 19th century novels I've read. <i>Diary of a Nobody</i> was the funniest; I wasn't as keen on <i>Three Men in a Boat</i> as some people are; those seem like the only real competition - 19th century classics perhaps not being notable for outright comedy (even if Dickens can be jovial). <i>Dead Souls</i> has a different kind of humour altogether, in a way more 20th century than 19th - black comedy with no real heroes - at least, in Part I, the finished section first published in 1842.<br /><br />Bernard Guerney's version was highly recommended, said by a number of academics, other translators and Russian native speakers to be the closest in English to Gogol's humour and spirit. Nabokov, too, said it was the only good one. I'm not sure that the sort of literal translation favoured by Nabokov produces the most readable texts in English, c.f. his <i>Eugene Onegin</i>, so I wouldn't automatically go with his choice - but about this edition, I think he was right. Just from reading samples of several translations, this one was different; it was actually funny; others raised the ghost of a smile, or were sort of funny in one's head if you thought about it hard enough. I feel like I've read a different book from many other English readers of <i>Dead Souls</i>, who haven't read it as a comic novel and have, frankly, missed out.<br /><br />Reading <i>Dead Souls</i> was more about enjoying the journey and the caper - as various comic characters try to rip each another off - than about mining for analysis, unlike a lot of the classics I've read this year. The strong presence of metafictional narrative, with its fluctuating tales of authorial control or lack of it, put me in mind of Fielding's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/261656223" rel="nofollow noopener">Tom Jones</a> (a detailed comparison would be geekily interesting but hard work). And repeating phrases and episodic structures in which a succession of scenes mirrored each other with variations - such as Chichikov visiting several landowners near N___ - indeed made it like 'a poem' (the novel's subtitle in many editions), echoing the oral tradition still alive among serfs. It was heartening to find a classic novel all about middle-aged characters - some years ago I remember being less keen to read more classics as it seemed as if most of them were all about people in their twenties or late teens getting married (though there is, of course, no shortage of 20th century novels about the middle-aged). The novel's observations about Russia are more or less on the surface, and I loved the myriad details of social history, which often raised more questions than it answered. (e.g. Would someone really sleep naked when it was quite cold weather and he was a gues? Was it common for the priest's wife to live in a separate house from the priest?)<br />As with a lot of Russian literature, there is self-mythologising and self-stereotyping of the country, and a great deal of it even by 19th century standards when many countries did this. Yet it wasn't quite the writing, but the cover of yet another edition - which I didn't read - <a href="https://www.amazon.co.uk/Dead-Souls-Oxford-Worlds-Classics/dp/0199554668" rel="nofollow noopener">Oxford</a>, a detail from Midday Near Moscow by Ivan Shishkin, which for the first time gave me some kind of felt sense of the vastness of Russia, that (in many places) however long you walked or took your cart, you'd still be in Russia - to paraphrase Pratchett, it's Russia all the way across, and how this sense of inescapability might affect the felt sense of life and its possibilities. <br /><br />It did, though, dovetail interestingly with a set of audio lectures on Greek mythology, which I mostly listened to the same month. There was a discussion of contemporary understanding of what a hero is (morally admirable) versus the ancient Greek idea (a larger-than-life character, and who, in many famous cases, was known for deeds that were taboo or immoral alongside those that moderns might consider heroic). Chichikov seemed closer to this Greek idea of the hero than that of the Christian era, in his ambiguity. As we learn in the final chapter of Part 1, he has a bootstraps tenacity with the ability to repeatedly work his way up the ladder and save money, and loyalty to ideas inherited from his father, who died when he was a boy - stuff beloved of American self-help. But he has absorbed some ideas and examples from the venial society around him too well. His example shows how easy it is to be corrupt in a corrupt society, and illustrates much that was wrong with Russian society of the time (aspects of which, some people who've lived there will argue still exist). Yet, unless one is incredibly strict about characters' morals, it is difficult not to root for him through most of the novel - and he is so very good at some things, and such a memorable and entertaining character, that he seems archetypal, like the Greek hero.<br /><br />The only drawback to the Guerney translation - other than its being less readily available in bookshops, at least in the UK, than a few other translations - is that it only contains Part I in full; short excerpts from Part II are in an appendix. Several GR friends who've read <i>Dead Souls</i> consider that Part II shouldn't be published for general consumption anyway, but relegated to academia as many authors' papers are, so if you're of that opinion and want a re-read, Guerney is ideal - or if you just want a shorter book. <br /><br />But I wanted to know what people were talking about when they talk about part II. So I read it in the recent Donald Rayfield translation, published in the US by NYRB Classics, and in the UK by Alma Classics; he includes includes a 'synoptic version' of Part II incorporating text from Gogol's two surviving drafts. (Rayfield is also recommended by Boyd Tonkin in his <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2846150361" rel="nofollow noopener">100 Best Novels in Translation</a>.) I also wanted to read it because it prefigures - or influenced - so much other Golden Age Russian literature; in Rayfield's words: <br />"Gogol’s paralysis when faced with the task of describing the uncompromising self-sacrificing heroine Ulinka, a type that was waiting for Turgenev and Dostoevsky to turn her into a role model for Russian womanhood) … Part 2, in any case, contains in embryonic form most major Russian fiction of the rest of the nineteenth century — Goncharov’s Oblomov, Turgenev’s Rudin, Dostoevsky’s penitent criminals, and Tolstoy’s heart-searching landowners."<br />They didn't all just come out of Gogol's Overcoat, they rose from Part II of <i>Dead Souls</i>. I haven't read Turgenev and Goncharov thus far, but in aspects of Gogol's Tentetnikov and Kostanzhoglo, Levin (<i>Anna Karenina</i>) is blatantly visible. Ulinka read to me like a 21st century middle-class student sounding off on Twitter about social justice issues - a woman before her time, but someone who would also be relevant to the explosion in political activism in later 19th century Russia. She seems a good deal more assertive and independent than the young women in <i>Crime and Punishment</i> - but the section of the manuscript has not survived in which she decides to follow her new young husband to Siberia.<br /><br />Thanks to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3046213430" rel="nofollow noopener">a conversation</a> with a GR friend, I realised that Krasznahorkai's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1147471618" rel="nofollow noopener">Satantango</a> is also indebted to <i>Dead Souls</i>: the long sentences, the mysterious swindlers arriving in a provincial area, the lack of conventional heroes. <i>Satantango</i> is if <i>Dead Souls</i> were as dark as its title suggests. (I thought it silly at first that several introductions mention the title being misleadingly dark, because I understood it was a comic novel - but later I realised the title had affected my decisions on when to read the book, and I hadn't imagined it to be quite the caper that Part 1 is.)<br /><br />Rayfield thinks that Part II is not as bad as a lot of people say it is: "I might also add that part 2 is neither as incomplete nor as uneven as the critical consensus asserts." I tend to agree. It isn't as good as Part I, but it has some lovely nature writing, and an interesting panorama of character types. It does fall down on three counts. It is not as witty - the excerpts of Part II in Guerney suggest that's true whoever the translator is, though Guerney's phrasing does make some scenes a little more humorous than Rayfield's. It explains too much - excerpts from a draft chapter of Part I in another appendix to the Guerney edition show that the final version of Part I made many things more mysterious, either until the last chapter, or full stop, and that this was what gave it its power. And Part II moralises more and lacks the fascinating ambiguity of Part I - though I thought it was still quite interesting with its morality, with character such as Kostanzhoglo and Murazov, rather in the way that Dickens can be; but it still seemed a more complex moral universe than Dickens' novels. It's also potentially disappointing because it was possible to read Part I as anti-system and anti-serfdom, whilst Part II seems somewhat clearer about Gogol's own opinions, which seemed to have been merely to reform serfdom rather than abolish it. Characters such as Kostanzhoglo and Murazov were very interesting in the context of Gogol himself. Was the author, a sickly man plagued with writers' block by the time he created them, a fan of these phenomenally hardworking, energetic and materially successful entrepreneur-paragons, whom he could never have managed to be - but perhaps wished he could be? Or was he eventually setting them up for criticism? His preoccupation with nature versus nurture in both parts of <i>Dead Souls</i>, coming down firmly on the side of nurture, hint at imagined possibilities of personal change, and at someone who'd spent a long time considering these questions on his own behalf. <br /><br />The combination of the two editions was perhaps most fruitful via reading the full Part II in Rayfield after the selected letters from Gogol in the final appendix to Guerney. Having read how the author was struggling, before reading the product of that time, it made me very sympathetic to the tortuous writing process, inclined to see these chapters more kindly, and left me pleasantly surprised that they weren't actually terrible. <br /><br />Rayfield's translation was in a style which was very enjoyable, but which I'm starting to suspect of being kind of generic. It had that comfortable yet awe-inspiring richness of detail, and cosiness combined with just the right amount of complexity, which felt very similar to all the other 19th century Russian novels I've read in 21st century translations. I found it rewarding to read on one level, but it meant Gogol felt a lot like Tolstoy and Dostoevsky and was not truly distinctive. In Guerney's more frenetic, funnier writing (the pace of action feels noticeably different between Guerney and Rayfield), he really did feel like a different author, and it was totally clear why Russians consider Gogol a great comic writer - whilst too many English reader-reviews of him - sadly - barely mention humour.<br /><br />Part I felt complete in itself, in the way that any first instalment of a novel series can; Part II is interesting but the final surviving pages seem somewhat tangled in their narrative, and it seems better not knowing its ending, in case it was worse. At any rate, it has made me keen to read more Gogol, though getting the right stories in an appealing translation (not P&amp;V!) seems scarcely more straightforward than it was with <i>Dead Souls</i>. The complexity, with the novel, was worth it, though, as it turned out to be so much fun. <br /><br />(Read November 2019, reviewed December 2019)</span>
  <a data-text-id="1472999534273424293" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_794921291" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/794921291" />
    <a title="Alan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5650849-alan"><img alt="Alan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1310234599p2/5650849.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/794921291?book_show_action=true">Dec 20, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alan" href="/user/show/5650849-alan">Alan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer794921291" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8841185555923766521">I have read only fifty pages of Gogol in Russian, enough to know how hilarious he is, and to regret his conversion and attempt to destroy this great book.<br/> "Sobakavich" alone rewards the reader with the Russian patronymic, "Son of" applied to "Sobaka," a bitch. Yet Sobakavich is the most genial of men, who refuses to sell even those of his employ who have died. His sentimental valuing of the mere memory of his dead worker is a triumph over materialism. Lovely stuff. Viva Gogol! Sobakievich is eve</span>
  <span id="freeText8841185555923766521" style="display:none">I have read only fifty pages of Gogol in Russian, enough to know how hilarious he is, and to regret his conversion and attempt to destroy this great book.<br /> "Sobakavich" alone rewards the reader with the Russian patronymic, "Son of" applied to "Sobaka," a bitch. Yet Sobakavich is the most genial of men, who refuses to sell even those of his employ who have died. His sentimental valuing of the mere memory of his dead worker is a triumph over materialism. Lovely stuff. Viva Gogol! Sobakievich is even more relevant today where tax evasion by the 1% is an industry supporting accountants and lawyers, supposed "business" experts who are really experts at short-changing the public.<br /> And American tax laws may now surpass those of 19th C Czarist Russia--in the loopholes provided for the rich--though in fact the loophole Chichikov exploits has not reemerged.<br /> Here it is: In Czarist Russia, wealth was not calculated by land. Anyone might own tens of thousands of hectares, or hundreds of square versts. You were not rich enough to marry unless you possessed the workers to till the land, the мужик. Of course, you owned them, but also, they had a right to till the land--not exactly like American slavery. Chichikov discovers a loophole in the tax law, so he plans to amass souls for his land, in order to marry well. The big house. The BMW, the коляска, the fancy carriage.<br /><br /> Back when I was reading Russian under the tutelage of a Bolshoi violinist, I decided to purchase Gogol for my shelves, and drove the hour and a half to Harvard Square, to Shoenhof's Books. They had no Мертвые души, but they had a later work, hardbound in green. I vaguely knew Gogol had repented his best writing, but I didn't think of the implication. A later work...hmmm. When I got home and started reading it, it turned out to be a kind of spiritual autobiography, the title roughly, Meditations on the Divine Legacy. I do not object to religion, though I think the 28,000 years light takes to arrive from the center of our own Milky Way Galaxy a humbling even of, say, Judaism's 5,000 year, and certainly Christianity's 2,000. I do not object, I just prefer not to read such personal submersion. Give me Rousseau, or Gogol before his conversion.<br /> In brief, we have outgrown Gogol on tax evasion and on slavery--NOT. I have always told my classes about Chaucer's attitude toward the alchemist who briefly joins the pilgrims, before he is "outed" as a fraud.<br /> Here 'tis: Mankind is not bright enough to muck around with chemistry and atoms. Why, we might blow ourselves up! I would add, Of course, Chaucer was Wrong--NOT.<br /> Now nobody reads Gogol's conversion rhetoric, but they read his great lit, IF they learn "Dead Souls" is a comedy, the worst-titled comedy. Viva Gogol! Viva lit!</span>
  <a data-text-id="8841185555923766521" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1128876044" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1128876044" />
    <a title="Srividya" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27599745-srividya"><img alt="Srividya" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1499431918p2/27599745.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1128876044?book_show_action=true">Apr 19, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Srividya" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Srividya" href="/user/show/27599745-srividya">Srividya</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/31818230-dead-souls">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27599745-srividya?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27599745-srividya?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27599745-srividya?shelf=kindle-owned">kindle-owned</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27599745-srividya?shelf=russian-lit">russian-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27599745-srividya?shelf=russia">russia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27599745-srividya?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27599745-srividya?shelf=2018-challenge">2018-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27599745-srividya?shelf=satire">satire</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27599745-srividya?shelf=world-lit-2018">world-lit-2018</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1128876044" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12798519804354973129">The weather was hot and humid and conducive for only one thing, sleeping. I had finished Dostoevksy’s The House of the Dead and was looking forward to relaxing and thinking about how to write a review for that book. However, the pull towards another Russian, a Russian that D admired and a book and its characters that D referred to consistently in his book was just too much of a temptation to me. I <i>had</i> to read the book and understand why D, one of my favourite authors, felt so moved and inspired </span>
  <span id="freeText12798519804354973129" style="display:none">The weather was hot and humid and conducive for only one thing, sleeping. I had finished Dostoevksy’s The House of the Dead and was looking forward to relaxing and thinking about how to write a review for that book. However, the pull towards another Russian, a Russian that D admired and a book and its characters that D referred to consistently in his book was just too much of a temptation to me. I <i>had</i> to read the book and understand why D, one of my favourite authors, felt so moved and inspired by it. Needless to say, he wasn’t the only one, as there have been a whole host of writers who have been inspired by this author and this book. The author is Nikolai Vasilievich Gogol; and the book is Dead Souls. <br /><br />Like a moth to a flame, almost sensuously attracted, without a thought of my own in my mind, I just knew that I <i>had</i> to read this one. I didn’t think about the heat or the need to sleep or even the fact that I hadn’t written a review. Without feeling any guilt or any kind of remorse for not continuing the books that I had already started, I started my journey with Gogol and his topic of Dead Souls. Fool that I was, I didn’t realise how much I would have changed by the end of this adventure, I just jumped in and swam without a thought.<br /><br />Every author at some point in his life wants to write a book or does write one where he puts in his heart and soul and talks about everything that he has always wanted to talk about but refrained from doing so in his earlier works. Dead Souls is Gogol’s that book where we can see every nuance of his thoughts, his deepest fears, his outlook on life, about Russia and her people and just about everything that he wanted to share with his readers. And he does share it all, at least most of it, almost as if he knew that it was time to say everything, and he just couldn’t restrict himself any more. And it is a good thing that he did write this one for it is truly a brilliant book and its brilliance lies not only in the thoughts that he has shared but also in the approach he took while sharing them. <br /><br />Our hero in this book, Pavel Ivanovich Chichikov, isn’t your usual quintessential hero who is filled with virtue and good looks and just about everything that is usually found in a <i>hero</i> that you find in most books. No, he isn’t that at all and yet he isn’t an anti-hero either. Gogol describes him as <i>“ a gentleman – not good looking but not uncomely in appearance either, not overly fat, nor overly thin. You couldn’t say he was old, yet you could not say he was overly young either.”</i> In other words, he is just a normal human being with his virtues and flaws, both in looks as well as in personality, and Gogol deliberately makes him the hero of this book. Gogol introduces us to hero, describing his entrance in the town, which remains unnamed and the other characters of the town in this poem, as he calls it, with a satirical tone that makes for many a laugh as you progress. <br /><br />Our hero, Chichikov, enters the town with very little splash but soon makes up for it when he goes around paying his respect to the various key members of the town. The town and its people embrace him heartily and welcome him with love and affection, little do they know of the devious nature of our <i>hero</i>. Chichikov becomes one of theirs and he slowly reveals his real intention to visiting their town, which was to purchase dead souls. In old Russia, peasants were treated as souls who could be bought and sold and even mortgaged along with the land. This practice I believe, has been long abolished, but was still prevalent when Gogol wrote this book. <br /><br />What follows once our hero attempts to acquire these dead souls is a tale that reveals at once the fallacies of humans along with their naivete, the depths to which some would fall in their greed for making money, the ambiguous nature of laws prevalent in those days, the politics of the country and finally the vivacity of the Russian society. Under Gogol’s dexterous hands we are immediately drawn into the plot, which is tragi-comical in nature and which does not let go even at the end. Gogol’s wit is exquisite throughout this part, where he describes Russian society and the quirks of small town Russia. Every character, irrespective of how big or small his or her role is, is perfectly rounded and reflects the diverse types of people you would encounter if you were to travel around Russia. And for that matter, why only Russia, am sure you would encounter such people everywhere in the world, except for the fact that instead of vodka and zakuskis you would find something else that binds them together. The entire first part of this book is definitely a laugh riot, where you travel throughout this unnamed town and meet its various characters through the eyes of our hero whilst also being witness to their reaction once they learn about the real intentions of our hero’s visit to their town. <br /><br />The second part, which unfortunately has many pages missing, is written quite differently by Gogol. Where satire marked the entire first part, this one is less satirical and more honest and rich in its descriptions of the Russian countryside. Gogol, in this part, has tried to create a vivid picture,<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <b>
    <i>“Like the gigantic rampart of some endless fortress, with angle towers and embrasures, the hill heights ran their winding way for a thousand versts and more. Majestically they soared above the endless stretches of plains, now in escarpments, sheer walls of lime and clay fretted by gullies and cavities, now in gracefully rounded green swellings cloaked in lambskin like young brushwood springing from the felled trees, now, finally in dark thickets of woodland, so far spared the axe by some miracle. A river, true to its banks, now followed them in turns and twists, now left them for the meadows, then, bending itself into several bends, flashed fire like in the sun, hid itself in groves of birch, aspen and alder, and ran forth from them in triumph, attended by bridges, mills and weirs, which seemed to chase after it at every turn.”</i>
  </b>
</blockquote><br /><br />And the pictures don’t end there but go on to describing the lands of the various landowners whilst also describing the landowners themselves, all vying with each other to present a picture that is at once radiant and beautiful. Chichikov, as is his due, meets with a variety of landowners in this part as well. His character gets more rounded as we go through the story and we can see his thoughts getting more definition and his acts becoming more brazen. However, before we can truly sink into this, we are abruptly asked to move onto the third part. <br /><br />The third part is more of a concluding chapter that was written based on the guidelines that were found for taking the story forward. Therefore, while this part can be called a part where the hero seeks redemption for all his sins, it is too jumpy a text for us to truly make out whether the hero truly sought redemption or does his behavior change momentarily because of the circumstances that he found himself in. However, even though it is mere points and guidelines, you still see the strength of Gogol’s vision for his hero, for humanity and the beauty of his words stand strong. The turn of mind towards Christianity that was forged during the years when he wrote, burned, rewrote the second and third part can be seen strongly through his language, ideas and choice of words. It was a different Gogol who wrote it. Was he a better Gogol? One cannot say as one doesn’t have the full facts to look at but what one cannot do is ignore it. What you cannot do is deny the intelligence of that mind and his far reaching, often visionary thoughts. <br /><br />In short, irrespective of whether the book has a full second and third part or not, its brilliance is such that you cannot deny it. This book contains all that you can ever encounter, whether it is the depths of their depravity or the heights of their innocence, their insouciance and laissez faire attitude in some areas or their involvement and their propensity for gossip in other areas; you come across all this and more when reading this poem. No subject is taboo for Gogol, be it religion, philosophy, government practices, politics, crime and just about anything is present here and is available in a form that hits hard, even when you don’t want it to and you cannot deny its relevance even in today's world. <br /><br />The overt and subtle sarcasm that fills this book makes you think and feel all those things that are usually left best swept under the carpet, to be looked at some other day, a day which hopefully never arrives. You feel honour bound to hate Chichikov and ridicule most of the characters in this book and feel like telling the author that he has created something that doesn’t exist, and yet you cannot do that for the author presupposes this reaction of yours and deals with it in such a manner that doing so would make you look ridiculous, especially when the author forces you to ask yourself, <i>“And isn’t there something of Chichikov in me too?”</i><br /><br />Chichikov and his travels around Russia, in his britska, stay with you even after you finish the book, and you can’t help but raise a silent toast to the master writer and acknowledge the truth, concerning humanity, concerning yourself, even if it is only silently and in your mind, for you have not the strength yet to speak out loud. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12798519804354973129" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3529475076" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3529475076" />
    <a title="Chrissie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie"><img alt="Chrissie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1321799585p2/632247.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3529475076?book_show_action=true">Sep 20, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chrissie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chrissie" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie">Chrissie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=russia">russia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=humor">humor</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=audible-uk">audible-uk</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=hf">hf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=2020-read">2020-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3529475076" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7969797707792635455">Honestly, I like this book, but not a whole lot, so I have settled on three rather than four stars.<br/><br/>I like the satirical humor—it isn’t nasty, it makes you laugh.<br/><br/>The prose doesn’t feel dated; it’s easy to follow. Constance Garnett is the translator.<br/><br/>I like very much the descriptions of people and places—the writing is lyrical in parts. Reading this, you get a strong sense of time and place—provincial Russia in the first half of the 19th century. The emancipation of the serfs had yet to take place;</span>
  <span id="freeText7969797707792635455" style="display:none">Honestly, I like this book, but not a whole lot, so I have settled on three rather than four stars.<br /><br />I like the satirical humor—it isn’t nasty, it makes you laugh.<br /><br />The prose doesn’t feel dated; it’s easy to follow. Constance Garnett is the translator.<br /><br />I like very much the descriptions of people and places—the writing is lyrical in parts. Reading this, you get a strong sense of time and place—provincial Russia in the first half of the 19th century. The emancipation of the serfs had yet to take place; the emancipation reform began in 1861. Prior to this, serfs were landowners’ property—they could be bought, sold, even mortgaged. The purchase and then mortgaging of serfs is what the central character is up to in this tale. Serfs who had died, still registered in the last census, are what he buys. When he buys them, previous owners need no long pay taxes for them and so he is hoping to get them really cheap or for nothing. With these dead serfs in his possession he can get money by mortgaging them at a bank. We observe how the landowners / serf owners behave. Some, cognizant of the demand for the serfs, set a high price on them. The eponymous “dead souls” has two meanings—the dead serfs and the dead souls of egotistical, selfish, avaricious landowners. The book is a criticism of the Russian middleclass. <br /><br />The large number of characters and the Russian names gave me some trouble. I mixed up characters now and then. I think it is harder to listen to this than to read it. You don’t pick the book up for character portrayal. Characters are as silhouettes: they represent different types of people. I do like that the central character is not drawn as a shining hero. <br /><br />The book reads as a string of episodes, which makes it disjointed. It is debated if it was fully completed. Sections of the manuscript are missing, and the ending is abrupt, in fact it stops in the middle of a sentence. <br /><br />I am definitely glad to have read this. It is a classic not to be missed. The descriptive writing is better than good. The humor is topnotch. I like very much that the message conveyed is delivered through humor rather than by means of a dry, didactic sermon. On the minus side, the plot doesn’t hold together properly, goes off in different directions and should have been tightened. <br /> <br />Nicholas Boulton narrates wonderfully. The narration is clear and read at a good speed. He uses different intonations for different characters, and he does this well, without excessive exaggeration. His pronunciation of a character’s name is not varied, this does help one recognize them. That two characters have similarly sounding names is what gave me the most trouble. This is not the narrator’s fault! Five stars for the audio narration.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7969797707792635455" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1745322987" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1745322987" />
    <a title="Gabrielle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6753400-gabrielle"><img alt="Gabrielle" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598976963p2/6753400.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1745322987?book_show_action=true">Dec 13, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gabrielle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gabrielle" href="/user/show/6753400-gabrielle">Gabrielle</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781847496287" href="/book/show/32710827-dead-souls">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=russian-soviet">russian-soviet</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=own-a-copy">own-a-copy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=read-in-2019">read-in-2019</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1745322987" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5522150519247842319">3 and a half stars, maybe it'll be rounded up, I have to think about it.<br/><br/>“Dead Souls” has a complicated reputation as a very important classic, but also as a potentially frustrating unfinished work. A certain understanding of the context in which Gogol wrote it is crucial to grasp the dark humour of the plot – not to mention the commentary Gogol was making on his society.<br/><br/>Chichikov is, for all intents and purposes, a con-man: as he lives in a society ruled by corrupt officials, he doesn’t really t</span>
  <span id="freeText5522150519247842319" style="display:none">3 and a half stars, maybe it'll be rounded up, I have to think about it.<br /><br />“Dead Souls” has a complicated reputation as a very important classic, but also as a potentially frustrating unfinished work. A certain understanding of the context in which Gogol wrote it is crucial to grasp the dark humour of the plot – not to mention the commentary Gogol was making on his society.<br /><br />Chichikov is, for all intents and purposes, a con-man: as he lives in a society ruled by corrupt officials, he doesn’t really think his moral conduct is all that reprehensible, and if anything, considers those who condemn him to be hypocrites who exploit others for their own gain just as much as he does, albeit, from behind a veil of legitimacy and lawfulness. As we follow him on his sordid quests to purchase the titular “dead souls”, we encounter all manners of characters that show the reader the crumbling of Russian society, as the ripples of the failed French Invasion continue to be felt across all social strata. Eventually and inevitably, his bad deeds catch up with him, but since the novel is unfinished, we don't really know what fate awaits him.<br /><br />In tone, this book is much closer to “The Master and Margarita” (<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1343219704">https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a>) than to “War &amp; Peace” (<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1343572901">https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a>), despite taking place in a time much closer to Tolstoy’s story than to Bulgakov’s (who, it must be noted, adapted “Dead Souls” for the stage in the 30s). But I'm afraid I kept waiting for it to get as good as either: perhaps it's incompleteness is to blame, but I finished the book feeling frustrated. The writing was often quite beautiful, and the characters vividly described - I supposed I simply would have liked to know how their stories ended. Does Chichikov get away with his madcap plan? Does he give up the scams and decides to go legit? I understand that closure is not necessarily the point of a good story, but "Dead Souls" was clearly meant to have an ending...<br /><br />It's an interesting experience, too, reading a surrealist and existential novel like this one when the world we currently live in has reached levels of surrealism and existentialism that most people probably didn’t expect to experience in their lifetimes. My sense of humour, which has admittedly always been rather dark and morbid, has not gotten much lighter, and I admit that laughing at the madness is sometimes the only way not to fall head first into it which makes Gogol’s absurd and picaresque story almost hysterical at times.<br /><br />So in the end, as I can't quite decide how I feel about it, but think it will merit a re-read in the future, I leave it at 3 undecided stars...</span>
  <a data-text-id="5522150519247842319" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1955674787" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10601922558120599366">Way ahead of its time, Dead Souls turned out to be everything I'd hoped, but still caught me a little by surprise as to just how funny it actually turned out to be. Blending realism and the picaresque, I read this when I went through a bit of an obsession for Russian literature. One could even argue Russian literature is the best literature, at least the older stuff anyway. The anti-hero Chichikov was a most memorable character, who simply travels around the Russian countryside buying up the dea</span>
  <span id="freeText10601922558120599366" style="display:none">Way ahead of its time, Dead Souls turned out to be everything I'd hoped, but still caught me a little by surprise as to just how funny it actually turned out to be. Blending realism and the picaresque, I read this when I went through a bit of an obsession for Russian literature. One could even argue Russian literature is the best literature, at least the older stuff anyway. The anti-hero Chichikov was a most memorable character, who simply travels around the Russian countryside buying up the dead souls of luckless peasants who were registered as alive at the last census but who have subsequently entered into the land of the dead. Chichikov, the naughty little schemer, plans to make tons of money out of this scam by defrauding the central government. He is charming, sociable, and very persuasive and bumps into all kinds of odd and eccentric landowners. Gogol's novel takes on a satirical element on the middle classes of the time, whilst his characterisation on human nature works a treat. Chichikov always seems to manage to get away with his outrageous antics by a hair's breadth, escaping just in time. Disappointingly It finishes mid sentence, which is a crying shame, and the final chapters are a tad anti climatic, after the interesting story appeared to be leading to a grand conclusion. Dead Souls is still a marvellous classic novel. A playful romp that exceeded my expectations.</span>

  <span id="freeText15190966582540017581" style="display:none">An entertaining satire published in 1842 but with flippant style that seems much more modern. Bureaucratic inept government and pompous neurotic gentry get scathing treatment. For example after a huge dinner at a noble’s estate, “…the master of the house had settled himself into his…armchair that would have held four, he dropped asleep. His corpulent person was transformed into a blacksmith’s bellows; from his open mouth and from his nose he began to emit sounds as are not found in even the newest music. <div id="review_672943441" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/672943441" />
    <a title="Roy Lotz" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14046996-roy-lotz"><img alt="Roy Lotz" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1443324405p2/14046996.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/672943441?book_show_action=true">Dec 22, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Roy Lotz" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Roy Lotz" href="/user/show/14046996-roy-lotz">Roy Lotz</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14046996-roy-lotz?shelf=novels-novellas-short-stories">novels-novellas-short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14046996-roy-lotz?shelf=russophilia">russophilia</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer672943441" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15195099522270820309"><blockquote>
  <i>There are, of course, many faces in the world over the finish of which nature has taken no great pains</i>
</blockquote><br/>For a book with such a gloomy title, this is quite a fun read. Gogol took the genre of the picaresque novel—born in Spain, and widely popular in England—and applied it to the Russia of the early 1800s. The result is a brilliant satire of his place and time. Our hero, Chichikov, rides around provincial Russia trying to buy up the deeds for dead peasants. This is a get-rich-quick scheme based </span>
  <span id="freeText15195099522270820309" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i>There are, of course, many faces in the world over the finish of which nature has taken no great pains</i>
</blockquote><br />For a book with such a gloomy title, this is quite a fun read. Gogol took the genre of the picaresque novel—born in Spain, and widely popular in England—and applied it to the Russia of the early 1800s. The result is a brilliant satire of his place and time. Our hero, Chichikov, rides around provincial Russia trying to buy up the deeds for dead peasants. This is a get-rich-quick scheme based on the absurdities of the Russia bureaucracy: namely, that all serfs (even dead ones) were counted as living until the next census was taken. (The idea is to use the serfs as leverage for a loan.)<br /><br />This serves as the perfect vehicle to push the middle-aged Chichikov—clever, strong-willed, but ultimately vain and petty—into contact with all sorts of provincial characters. Gogol shows his greatest talent in drawing this gallery of people, each of them representative of a archetype, and yet each of them believably individualized. What emerges is a picture of Russia more vivid than any I know, full of living detail and told in sharp and lively prose.<br /><br />What interests me about this novel is that it shows how art can be totally independent of any message or philosophy—and that, despite the wishes of its own author. On its face it is tempting to read the book (or at least the first part) as a kind of satire on the institution of serfdom and the dysfunction of the Russian state. But the book will not sustain this reading. For one, Gogol was for serfdom, and the serfs themselves hardly play a role in this book. What is more, the whole scheme of buying dead souls, while amusing, is never the main attraction. Gogol is no reformer, at least not in the conventional sense. He is attracted to distinctive human types; and the book becomes more about the foibles of personalities rather than anything social or cultural.<br /><br />Even more curious is that Gogol’s creative power broke down when he decided to convert this novel into a grand philosophical statement. This happened after he finished Part One, when he decided that he had a kind of messianic mission to save Russia. Part Two and Three would be equivalents to the <i>Purgatorio</i> and the <i>Paradiso</i> of the <i>Divine Comedy</i>, showing Chichikov turning away from vice and towards a properly Russian virtue. The strain of this heroic task eventually broke Gogol, it seems, and he burned his manuscripts and then starved himself.<br /><br />What survives of Part Two, while admittedly only a draft, is considerably weaker than the first part. The focus on sharp personality is gone, and instead there are many long speeches about the virtue of being a productive landowner, the native power of the Russian culture, and so forth. Gogol would have Russians turn away from everything European and become serf-owning farmers cultivating their own gardens. This makes for unconvincing philosophy and stale writing. Nevertheless, I think this is quite interesting to see. For it shows that, contrary to what we often take for granted nowadays, a social mission is not exactly a good thing for a novelist. Gogol was more deeply incisive when he was doing it for the sake of creating an entertaining book.<br /><br />And so Part One, which Gogol thought would be seen as a mere prelude to the profound message of the later works, is considered to be his defining vision.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15195099522270820309" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_789268152" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/789268152" />
    <a title="Tej" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5074564-tej"><img alt="Tej" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1460673152p2/5074564.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/789268152?book_show_action=true">Dec 13, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tej" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tej" href="/user/show/5074564-tej">Tej</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Everyone </span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5074564-tej?shelf=all-time-favorites">all-time-favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer789268152" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10872290453957104659">Almost, one and a three quarters of a century ago, Nikolay Vasilyevich Gogol-Yanovsky or simply, Gogol, himself lend words to the cries of dissent against the likes of him,<br/><br/><i>“Don’t we ourselves know that there’s much in life that’s contemptible and stupid? As it is, we often have occasion to see things that are far from comforting. Better that you show us what’s beautiful and distracting. Better that we should forget ourselves!”</i><br/><br/>That very arrogance and contempt has rocketed far beyond, eulogizing a</span>
  <span id="freeText10872290453957104659" style="display:none">Almost, one and a three quarters of a century ago, Nikolay Vasilyevich Gogol-Yanovsky or simply, Gogol, himself lend words to the cries of dissent against the likes of him,<br /><br /><i>“Don’t we ourselves know that there’s much in life that’s contemptible and stupid? As it is, we often have occasion to see things that are far from comforting. Better that you show us what’s beautiful and distracting. Better that we should forget ourselves!”</i><br /><br />That very arrogance and contempt has rocketed far beyond, eulogizing all that is trash and sadly, maybe for the very reason that it is trash. The measure of cinema, at least here in my part of the world is by the millions that it rakes in irrespective of the nature of content, millions are proportional to the trash really or there is that noise which must be called music, not to mention the TV. We all are a part of that and beyond a point not willful perpetrators but just so hard pressed by life that there is little or no time to bother. It struck me the most, Gogol’s desire to produce something of significance, with a potential inclination towards inculcating the seeds of change among the individuals, indirectly goading and exhorting them and to somehow impact positively their lives and times. His self-assessed inability to achieve that coveted goal in writing, to an extent, led him to inflict self-damage by starvation and ultimate demise not to mention the burnings he carried out of his manuscript more than once. The first part of ‘Dead Souls’ leaves you most pleasantly dumbfounded and marveling at the precocious genius of this artist who paints human beings in prose, smiling and even positively laughing alone along with him and then, the second part of the same, incomplete and inconclusive, concluded his life journey quite literally. You wonder, why not just to continue writing with the kind of talent you were bestowed with? But then that is you talking in 2013 where ‘callings’ and ‘inner-voices’ are the last things to determine what and when you do what. Sadly I got to read Gogol saying this,<br /><br /><i>“God had taken away ‘for a long time my ability to create’, as a result of which ‘I have tormented myself, forced myself to write, suffered painfully at the spectacle of my impotence, and several times have made myself ill with the strain and have been unable to accomplish anything, and everything has come out forced and bad.’ ”</i><br /><br />Pavel Ivanovich ‘Chichikov’, our heroic anti-hero cajoles us to accompany him, on pretty much aimless travels he has undertaken only driven by a supposedly ingenious yet untested and unproven idea of taking advantage of a loophole in the system, which putatively shall translate into real capital and a consequent plum life for his self. He is in fact on the mission to swindle bounty out of the system, keeping low and warding of the reach of law till he attains an un-approachable respectability in the society. He has the gumption to enshroud this trickery in a veil of un-burdening the prospective clients of the burden of ‘dead souls’ or the dead serfs, the tax one is obliged to pay because these serfs are counted as alive on the rolls with no chance of them being ticked of as dead until the subsequent census. He offers to even pay for them, peanuts that is, an item that exists only in thin air and aspires to mortgage them as real serfs while becoming a land-owner himself. Armed with an utterly ingratiating and ‘toady’ character, he ventures thus on his endeavours,<br /><br /><i>“My life can be likened, as it were, to a barque amid the waves, Your Excellency. I was swaddled, and one could say, wrapped in forbearance, myself being, so to speak, forbearance itself.”</i><br />……….<br /><i>“Somehow the new arrival was never at a loss for anything, and he came across as an experienced man of the world. Whatever the topic of conversation, he always knew how to hold up his end: if the talk was of stud farm, he too would talk of stud farms; if people were chatting of fine dogs, here too he would venture some very sensible observations; if the matter under consideration touched upon an investigation being conducted by the fiscal chamber, he showed that he was not ignorant of judicial hanky-panky; if the discussion turned to billiards, he didn’t let his end down when it came to billiards either; if people were talking about virtue, then he could discourse on virtue very well too, and even with tears in his eyes; if about the distilling of spirits, then he knew a lot about spirits as well; if about custom inspectors and officials, then he could also expiate on them as if he himself had been both an official and an inspector.”</i><br /><br />The rustic humor of the plot, in the dexterous hands of Gogol, is plied into a tragi-comic satire, one that embraces tightly, ingrained with a power to land hard blows with laughter. Gogol himself appears during the course of narration, monologues and casual talks flow, all of which happens in the ‘meanwhile’ in real time that is, which makes the text delightful. So much so, that even the carriage horses have their say at times. <br /><br /><i>“With us it’s different: we have men so wise that with a landowner who has two hundred souls they will speak in an altogether different way than with one who has three hundred, and with one who has three hundred they will, again, speak otherwise than with one who has five hundred, and with one who has five hundred, again it will be otherwise than with one who has eight hundred; in a word, you can get upto a million, and shadings will still be found.”</i><br /><br />And then there is this landowner Chichikov meets in his quest to secure ‘dead souls’ whom he ends up describing thus,<br /><br /><i>“His smile was alluring, his hair blond and his eyes light blue. In the first moment of conversation with him, you could not help but say, ‘What a pleasant and kind hearted man!’ The second moment you would say nothing, and the third you would say, ‘The devil only knows what sort of man he is!’ and you would move as far away from him as you could; if you didn’t move away, you would experience a feeling of deadly boredom.”</i><br /><br />Observing another lady in one of the landowner’s house, he goes ahead with,<br /><br /><i>“And in boarding schools, as we know, three main subjects constitute the foundation of human virtues: the French language, which is indispensable for a happy family life; the piano, for affording one’s spouse some pleasant moments; and finally, in the specifically homemaking skills, the knitting of purses and other surprises.”</i><br /><br />Oscar Wilde famously said,<i> “If you want to tell people the truth, make them laugh, otherwise they’ll kill you”</i> and Gogol epitomized that perfectly in trying to use comedy as a means towards perfecting individuals, one at a time with an ultimate aim to let that percolate and spread inside the entrails of the country, hopefully enlightening it as a whole. This contrasted starkly with the radicals trying to improve through systemic changes in social structures and government systems. So glaringly, this is the way to go even today and the need all the more acute.<br /><br />Flowing descriptions of the pulchritude of Russian countryside, adorn the second part where Gogol wields a different quill albeit without giving up his knack of humor a wee bit. At times the brilliance is much more vivid and writing candid here than the first part even but only to be rudely reminded of its incompleteness and then it strikes as a disjointed piece on the whole. Perhaps to achieve perspicacity, he forays into the uncharted and flounders, dabbling with morality here and sermons there which do not quite gel with the tone and tenor hitherto attained and that is tragic. <br /><br /><i>“Like a tsar on the day of his solemn coronation, he was all aglow and it seemed as if rays of light were streaming from his face. Why, nowhere in the whole world will you find a delight to equal that. It’s precisely here that man imitates God. God reserved to himself the business of creation, as a delight second to none, and he demands of man that he too, in like measure, be the creator of prosperity all around him. And they call this boring work!”</i><br />......<br /><i> “Yes, nature loves patience, and this is a law given it by God himself, who smiles on those who are patient.” (Reminds of Tolstoy)</i><br /><br />In the final sections, which happen so strangely suddenly, if one is not aware of the fate of this text and its creator, one, to say the least can be left utterly dumbfounded. <br /><br /><i>“….. Why such a fate? Why such blows? Was my life not like a barque amid the waves even without all that? Where is the justice of the heavens? Where is the reward for patience, for exemplary perseverance?”</i><br />…….<br /><i>“I can see, I can sense….. That the life I’m leading is not right, but I fell no great revulsion towards vice; my nature has grown coarse. I have no love for the good, for that beautiful inclination for doing deeds that are pleasing to God, which becomes second nature, habit.”</i><br />…….<br /><i>“The dishonest business of taking bribes has become a necessity and a need even for those who were certainly not born to be dishonest. I know by now it is almost impossible for many people to swim against the current.”</i><br /><br />My own naiveté as regards this work receded only gradually after the reading culminated and shoved me into an earnest quest to know the man himself. My only claims as to the knowledge of this work were cursory references of Dostoevsky and the likes, and I somehow expected it to be miles away from humour let alone comedy forming heart of the matter. I did not know what to expect and that was quite good actually. <br />Gogol’s life and times are enshrouded in dense fog with only his prolific letter writing providing a glimpse into the man and the artist that he was. Pushkin, evidently regarded as a mentor of had a famous falling apart and the two did not talk until Pushkin died in a duel. According to Gogol, it was Pushkin who visualized the ‘plot’ of Dead Souls and decided not to use it himself, encouraging him to amplify and deepen it. And Pushkin argued and coaxed him saying that it was just sinful not to do so, given <b>
  <i>the ability of Gogol to put his finger on a person and represent him fully as a human being in just a few strokes</i>
</b>. Moreover he pushed him to emulate Cervantes, to rise above the scope of smaller works which he was currently writing and produce a work of the character and stature of Don Quixote while overcoming the shortcomings that his frail and capricious health represented. Later Gogol is believed to have said, <br /><br /><i>“ ‘Service’ to Russia, he said, was his abiding concern, and to that end he ‘wanted to present in my work primarily those higher qualities of the Russian nature, which have not yet been justly appreciated by all, and primarily those lower qualities which have not yet been sufficiently ridiculed and dispelled by all’.”</i><br /><br />Gogol, right from the time of his first writings had a difficult time with criticism, burning text after text even only at slightest behest. His eccentricity and quirkiness culminated with his own life but it can be safely construed that in the present day and age, when goals are secondary, his prodigious precocity as a tragi-comic painter of realities of existence would have levitated him to precipitous heights. Even without that, his role and place in the echelons of Russian or even world writing traditions is incontrovertible, the evidences are replete in Tolstoy, Gorky or Dostoevsky and even Chekhov, who became who they did because he had been…..<br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10872290453957104659" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
